    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }

      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer1590927415432718536">In 1935, with a doctorate in art history and no prospect of a job, the 26-year-old Ernst Gombrich was invited by a publishing acquaintance to attempt a history of the world for younger readers. Amazingly, he completed the task in an intense six weeks, and <i>Eine kurze Weltgeschichte für junge Leser</i> was published in Vienna to immediate success, and is now available in sevente</span>
  <span id="freeText1590927415432718536" style="display:none">In 1935, with a doctorate in art history and no prospect of a job, the 26-year-old Ernst Gombrich was invited by a publishing acquaintance to attempt a history of the world for younger readers. Amazingly, he completed the task in an intense six weeks, and <i>Eine kurze Weltgeschichte für junge Leser</i> was published in Vienna to immediate success, and is now available in seventeen languages across the world. Toward the end of his long life, Gombrich embarked upon a revision and, at last, an English translation. <i>A Little History of the World</i> presents his lively and involving history to English-language readers for the first time. Superbly designed and freshly illustrated, this is a book to be savored and collected. In forty concise chapters, Gombrich tells the story of man from the stone age to the atomic bomb. In between emerges a colorful picture of wars and conquests, grand works of art, and the spread and limitations of science. This is a text dominated not by dates and facts, but by the sweep of mankind's experience across the centuries, a guide to humanity's achievements and an acute witness to its frailties. The product of a generous and humane sensibility, this timeless account makes intelligible the full span of human history.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1590927415432718536" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

      </div>
</div>

      $('edition_11570674').observe('prototip:shown', function() {
        if (this.up('#box')) {
          $$('div.prototip').each(function(i){i.setStyle({zIndex: $('box').getStyle('z-index')})});
        } else {
          $$('div.prototip').each(function(i){i.setStyle({zIndex: 6000})});
        }
      });

      newTip['wrapper'].addClassName('prototipAllowOverflow');

        $('edition_11570674').observe('prototip:shown', function () {
          $$('div.prototip').each(function (e) {
            if ($('edition_11570674').hasClassName('ignored')) {
              e.setStyle({'display': 'none'});
              return;
            }
            e.setStyle({'overflow': 'visible'});
          });
        });
      $('edition_11570674').observe('prototip:hidden', function () {
        $$('span.elementTwo').each(function (e) {
          if (e.getStyle('display') !== 'none') {
            var lessLink = e.next();
            swapContent(lessLink);
          }
        });
      });

      $('span_class_gr-hyperlink_sort_order_span_tip_3').observe('prototip:hidden', function () {
        $$('span.elementTwo').each(function (e) {
          if (e.getStyle('display') !== 'none') {
            var lessLink = e.next();
            swapContent(lessLink);
          }
        });
      });

//]]>
</script>    </div>

<div id="review_44491254" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/44491254" />
    <a title="David" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/166376-david"><img alt="David" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1345482402p2/166376.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/44491254?book_show_action=true">Jan 27, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="David" class="user" itemprop="url" name="David" href="/user/show/166376-david">David</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780300143324" href="/book/show/3711671-a-little-history-of-the-world">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/166376-david?shelf=read-in-2009">read-in-2009</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer44491254" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2203195880055180722">I can't bring myself to jump on the warm and fuzzy bandwagon of approval of this deeply flawed book. It is what it is - a condensation of all of human history into sequential "stories" suitable for "children". Supposing for the moment that this is not an inherent recipe for disaster, what is baffling is the number of reviewers who claim to see something in this work "for adults". <br/><br/>Other reviewers seem to agree on the book's "lack of condescension", so I guess I'll have to chalk my own perception </span>
  <span id="freeText2203195880055180722" style="display:none">I can't bring myself to jump on the warm and fuzzy bandwagon of approval of this deeply flawed book. It is what it is - a condensation of all of human history into sequential "stories" suitable for "children". Supposing for the moment that this is not an inherent recipe for disaster, what is baffling is the number of reviewers who claim to see something in this work "for adults". <br /><br />Other reviewers seem to agree on the book's "lack of condescension", so I guess I'll have to chalk my own perception of Gombrich's condescending tone up to an inability to put myself in the shoes of a 10-year old. So I'll give him the benefit of the doubt on that point.<br />(ON EDIT: hell, no I won't; I found him condescending as all get out) <br /><br />What I can't forgive him is the utterly Eurocentric, theistic worldview that permeates every page of this book. Oh sure, Buddha and Confucius get a token few pages, Islam gets its chapter. A typical excerpt: "I am especially grateful to the Arabs.... for the wonderful tales they used to tell... which you can read in 1,001 Nights." To be fair, he does give the Arabs credit for development of our modern number system, though he somewhat spoils the effect by continuing "Perhaps it's just as well that Charles Martel defeated the Arabs in 732".<br /><br />And so it goes. Want to know what happened to the native peoples of the Americas? "There (in Mexico) and in other parts of America, the Spaniards proceeded to exterminate the ancient, cultivated Indian peoples in the most horrendous way. This chapter in the history of mankind is so appalling and so shameful to us Europeans that I would rather not say anything more about it". So, not a mention of the human toll associated with Manifest Destiny (sorry, Plains Indians!); for the colonial depredations in Africa, just one sentence: "as you can imagine, the native inhabitants were often very badly treated if any of them tried shooting at the invading troops with their bows and arrows". <br /><br />Is this dude for real? I think there's a whole lot of Congolese (to mention just one example) who had their hands chopped off for "infractions" considerably less severe than shooting at their colonial invaders with bows and arrows. I suppose European behavior in Africa was just so shameful, best just not to mention it. Wouldn't want to upset the kids. <br /><br />For all I know, kids may lap this kind of thing up. But I doubt it. If this enormously biased, sanitized, middle Eurocentric view of history were all they were to be exposed to, the world would be in a sorry state indeed. </span>
  <a data-text-id="2203195880055180722" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_125160084" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/125160084" />
    <a title="Whitaker" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1415047-whitaker"><img alt="Whitaker" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1410926609p2/1415047.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/125160084?book_show_action=true">Oct 07, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Whitaker" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Whitaker" href="/user/show/1415047-whitaker">Whitaker</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1415047-whitaker?shelf=history">history</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1415047-whitaker?shelf=z_2010-read">z_2010-read</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer125160084" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4938095471036028633">Even if everything else in this book had been badly written (which is not the case), for this comment alone this book deserves very single one of the five stars I gave it: <blockquote>And when, in 1918, America's President Wilson announced that he wanted a just peace in which each nation would determine its own fate, many of their troops gave up....Then, having understood from President Wilson that there was to be a peace treaty, and that negotiations were to be held in the ancient royal palaces of Versaill</blockquote></span>
  <span id="freeText4938095471036028633" style="display:none">Even if everything else in this book had been badly written (which is not the case), for this comment alone this book deserves very single one of the five stars I gave it: <blockquote>And when, in 1918, America's President Wilson announced that he wanted a just peace in which each nation would determine its own fate, many of their troops gave up....Then, having understood from President Wilson that there was to be a peace treaty, and that negotiations were to be held in the ancient royal palaces of Versailles, St Germain and the Trianon, Austria, Hungary and Germany sent envoys to Paris, only to discover that they were excluded from these negotiations....So this was how President Wilson kept his promises. (What you have just read is what I believed to be true when I wrote this account, but read my explanation in the final chapter of this book.)<br />...<br />The role played by America's President Wilson was not at all what I had imagined. I described a situation in which Wilson made promises to the Germans and Austrians which he failed to keep. I firmly believed that what I remembered had to be right -- after all, it was part of my own experience -- and when I wrote about it later I just wrote down what everyone believed. <i>But I should have checked my facts, as all historians must be especially careful to do.</i> To cut a long story short, President Wilson did indeed make a peace offer early in 1918, but because Germany and Austria and their allies were still hoping to win the war, they ignored it. Only when the war had dragged on for ten more months, and they had been defeated with very heavy losses, were they prepared to accept the President's proposal. But by then it was too late.</blockquote> Gombrich first wrote this book in Germany in 1935. The edition I read was the English edition revised and prepared by Gombrich sometime between 1990 and 2005. He could easily have simply revised the first paragraph but he kept it the way it was first written, both preserving and admitting his mistake. Written primarily for schoolchildren, I think these two passages teach kids the most important lesson about history and about life: Check your facts and if you got it wrong, revise your beliefs and don't be afraid to admit it. In this day and age of truthiness, I think this one lesson is worth Glenn Beck’s weight in gold. <br /><br />On to the meat of the book... As the first quoted passage itself demonstrates, calling this book <i>A Little History of the World</i> is probably a little fanciful. It would be more accurate if it came with a subtitle: "As written by a German as a textbook for German schoolchildren". The viewpoint is European; more accurately, it is of a German addressing German children. So, European and Germanic affairs occupy a somewhat more central place in the book than the title itself would suggest. For example, he spends five pages talking about Frederick II of Hohenstaufen, ruler of Germany and Italy in the 13th century. This takes up more than half of one entire chapter (his chapters are very short), of which there are a total of 40 in the book. On the other hand, the Babylonian captivity merits barely a paragraph, and the Hundred Years War is dealt with in one-and-a-half pages. England's King Henry II and Eleanor of Acquitane, who arguably had a far greater impact on European history than 'ole Frederick, are not even mentioned.<br /><br />Is it a great history book for kids? I think it's perfectly decent introduction as long as you bear in mind its bias and viewpoint. It covers a large swath of human history in a fairly lively way for young children.* Adults might find the brevity and lightness of treatment either somewhat condescending or irritating, but then they are not the intended audience. To criticise it as such would be like criticising <i>Sponge Bob Square Pants</i> for being jejeune. That's true, but also somewhat besides the point. <br /><br />It was a fun read for me and I liked how this condensed approach allowed me to get an easy-to-read bird’s eye view of critical historical points and how they linked up with each other. More scholarly or adult oriented histories are best, but they can be heavy-going and the mass of detail can be hard to handle. <br /><br />So, those looking for a history of the world should really look elsewhere. If anyone knows of a single easy-reading book that covers the various sections of the world in equal measure throughout, I’d be thrilled to know about it. It does seem to be a book that is missing in the market though. <br /><br /><br />* A few GRs have criticised this book for not acknowledging more fully and in greater detail the suffering of native peoples in the Americas and other colonised lands. For example, he deals with the record of the Spanish in South America simply by stating, “…the Spaniards proceeded to exterminate the ancient, cultivated Indian peoples in the most horrendous way.” To be fair to Gombrich, it should be noted that he deals as sparsely (if not more so) with the Holocaust. As a Jew who escaped Germany before the worst arrived, he had all the reason in the world to play up this episode, but he doesn't. So if he glosses over this, his treatment of the history of Amerindians and black slavery is not likely to be due to some innate prejudice. In fact, he does expressly explain that as the book was directed at children, he felt it was inappropriate to dwell too much on such events. One can disagree with his judgement call, but his reason for it is reasonable. <br /><br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="4938095471036028633" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2420948989" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2420948989" />
    <a title="Jennifer" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2473504-jennifer"><img alt="Jennifer" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1394406877p2/2473504.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2420948989?book_show_action=true">Jun 11, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jennifer" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jennifer" href="/user/show/2473504-jennifer">Jennifer</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780300143324" href="/book/show/3711671-a-little-history-of-the-world">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2473504-jennifer?shelf=own-paperbook">own-paperbook</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2473504-jennifer?shelf=history">history</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2473504-jennifer?shelf=children-middle-grade">children-middle-grade</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2473504-jennifer?shelf=young-adult-genre">young-adult-genre</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2473504-jennifer?shelf=nonfiction">nonfiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2473504-jennifer?shelf=read-2018">read-2018</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2473504-jennifer?shelf=read-with-t-09th-grade">read-with-t-09th-grade</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2420948989" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11964632359929866065">I have always enjoyed reading books along with my son. He enjoyed the adult attention that came with buddy-reading when he was little, but now that we're in the teen years the motives seemed to have reversed. He humors me these days, thus the tradition of the annual summer read-along continues. Thank you, son. <br/><br/><a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/61505.A_Little_History_of_the_World' title='A Little History of the World by E.H. Gombrich' rel='nofollow noopener'>A Little History of the World</a> by <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/167112.E_H__Gombrich' title='E.H. Gombrich' rel='nofollow noopener'>E.H. Gombrich</a> was on his list for required summer reading this year (2018), so I picked up a copy as well and we went at it. He loves history, and as such,</span>
  <span id="freeText11964632359929866065" style="display:none">I have always enjoyed reading books along with my son. He enjoyed the adult attention that came with buddy-reading when he was little, but now that we're in the teen years the motives seemed to have reversed. He humors me these days, thus the tradition of the annual summer read-along continues. Thank you, son. <br /><br /><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/61505.A_Little_History_of_the_World" title="A Little History of the World by E.H. Gombrich" rel="nofollow noopener">A Little History of the World</a> by <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/167112.E_H__Gombrich" title="E.H. Gombrich" rel="nofollow noopener">E.H. Gombrich</a> was on his list for required summer reading this year (2018), so I picked up a copy as well and we went at it. He loves history, and as such, I think he wanted a bit more from this book. For me, it was a great refresher of a wide variety of well-known leaders and events, and I loved how the author (who lived through both world wars) inserted his own thoughts and personal observations into sections. <br /><br />Austrian born, Gombrich originally wrote this debut in his native German language and translated it himself years later. He also edited it for accuracy but kept the original text to show how views on history can shift or be mistaken. Note: I trashed the remainder of my intended review as I later stumbled across a review by <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/user/show/1415047-whitaker" rel="nofollow noopener">Whitaker</a> who had previously shared my exact thoughts on this reading experience (and communicated them far better than I ever could). See Whitaker's review <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/125160084" rel="nofollow noopener">HERE</a>. <br /><br />Keeping in mind this book was originally intended as an introduction to history written for younger readers in Europe, I would recommend it. I enjoyed the writing style, the content, and the personal contributions. My son is reading it for a comparative government class and I can absolutely see why it was selected. Leadership and what that looks like has evolved so much over time. Through war and peace and selfishness to protectiveness, we learn from history: what worked and what didn't. I'm looking forward to seeing what other history assignments of substance walk through our door.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11964632359929866065" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1689692085" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1689692085" />
    <a title="Lyn Elliott" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/10312986-lyn-elliott"><img alt="Lyn Elliott" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1377589438p2/10312986.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1689692085?book_show_action=true">Jul 06, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lyn Elliott" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lyn Elliott" href="/user/show/10312986-lyn-elliott">Lyn Elliott</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780300143324" href="/book/show/3711671-a-little-history-of-the-world">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10312986-lyn-elliott?shelf=europe">europe</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10312986-lyn-elliott?shelf=history">history</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10312986-lyn-elliott?shelf=germany">germany</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1689692085" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12141951612590192192">All the histories I read in school were based on an English world view - and yes it was English, because the Scots, Irish and Welsh barely got a look in except as being a nuisance needing to be put down. They certainly didn't give any idea about European history, as the island viewpoint prevailed (still does, it seems, after the Brexit vote).<br/>So reading Ernst Gombrich's Little History has been a delight. Lucky children to have this as an introduction to history. Of course the central viewpoint is</span>
  <span id="freeText12141951612590192192" style="display:none">All the histories I read in school were based on an English world view - and yes it was English, because the Scots, Irish and Welsh barely got a look in except as being a nuisance needing to be put down. They certainly didn't give any idea about European history, as the island viewpoint prevailed (still does, it seems, after the Brexit vote).<br />So reading Ernst Gombrich's Little History has been a delight. Lucky children to have this as an introduction to history. Of course the central viewpoint is Germany and the German speaking world, but it ranges far afield, to China and the Americas for instance. <br />It's extraordinary that Gombrich wrote it in only 6 weeks not long after he had finished his doctoral dissertation on art history, where he was to make his reputation as a brilliant scholar and communicator. Here he showed he could link big ideas together and explain them fairly simply- what economic crisis means, the German and Italian battle for colonies in the late nineteenth century (new nations pushing and shoving to join the other imperial powers) - and gives snapshots of how rich and poor people lived at different stages, their clothes, their work and their dwellings. <br />There were many occasions when I twitched at the way something was portrayed -the French Revolution for instance - but in the scheme of things I'm not sure how much this matters now. If you want accurate detail, you need to look elsewhere. Its strengths are in the range of geographical focus; the pressure points of war, revolution and famine; flows of social and political change and the sweep of ideas and the presence of art and design as part of life, which doesn't usually appear in general history books . <br />The writing is lively, the voice that of a storyteller.<br />The first edition, in German, was published in 1936, the second German edition not till the 1990s and the first English translation with a when it was relaunched with some revisions and a final chapter in which Gombrich looks back over events in his own life. Here he corrects his earlier interpretation of the American role in the Versailles Treaty that ended WWI, using his own error in interpretation as a example of why it's important that historians always check their facts. Here too he expresses his dismay at the intolerances and cruelties that marked the twentieth century and restates his 1936 expression of hope that things will be brighter in the future.<br />The first English translation appeared only in 2005, with a preface by his granddaughter Leonie Gombrich and wonderful line illustrations by Clifford Harper.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12141951612590192192" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

The book is intended for young readers - between 12 and 16 I would mean but is also enjoyable for the older reader - like myself - as a brief overview of what has happened on our planet from prehistoric times until the fall of the Berlin wall.   <span id="freeText14441581305004717599" style="display:none">A fantastic tour de force of the history of the world in less than 300 pages. The book is intended for young readers - between 12 and 16 I would mean but is also enjoyable for the older reader - like myself - as a brief overview of what has happened on our planet from prehistoric times until the fall of the Berlin wall.     <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_3130876272" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3130876272" />
    <a title="Scarlett Readz and Runz....Through Novel Time &amp; Distance" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/53419883-scarlett-readz-and-runz-through-novel-time-distance"><img alt="Scarlett Readz and Runz....Through Novel Time &amp; Distance" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1600345081p2/53419883.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3130876272?book_show_action=true">May 18, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Scarlett Readz and Runz....Through Novel Time &amp; Distance" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Scarlett Readz and Runz....Through Novel Time &amp; Distance" href="/user/show/53419883-scarlett-readz-and-runz-through-novel-time-distance">Scarlett Readz and Runz....Through Novel Time &amp; Distance</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3130876272" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9421950187992961426">Sir Ernst Hans Josef Gombrich was mainly known by his work "The Story of Art" which happens to be the book of his that fell into my lap first, though "A Little History of The World" was actually an earlier written work of his that was published way later in his life. <br/><br/>What attracted me to this novel was my search in finding something (a few years back) that could present an overview of world history in a fairly short storytelling format for a younger audience with just a little bit of grit that w</span>
  <span id="freeText9421950187992961426" style="display:none">Sir Ernst Hans Josef Gombrich was mainly known by his work "The Story of Art" which happens to be the book of his that fell into my lap first, though "A Little History of The World" was actually an earlier written work of his that was published way later in his life. <br /><br />What attracted me to this novel was my search in finding something (a few years back) that could present an overview of world history in a fairly short storytelling format for a younger audience with just a little bit of grit that would invite more questions into the subject matter and focus mainly on the Euroasian continents. <br /><br />I believe this book is available online in the audiobook format, and years ago it was my first introduction to this book before I had a physical copy in hand. I'm not certain if it was narrated by Sir Gombrich himself, but I do remember that it starts off very captivating for young listeners, hence why I bought the novel sometime later. <br /><br />There are a few things that might be noteworthy when thinking of giving this novel a try. For one, it is almost 100 years old. Sir Gombrich was 26 years old, of Austrian descent, with a Jewish family background when he wrote his novel in 6 weeks. It published with the title: 'Eine kurze Weltgeschichte fuer junge Leser' in 1935 and became an immediate success but also a banned book by the Nazis in the following years. <br />Another more obvious detail is that it isn't told from the perspective of any victors in history but heavily reflects on the humanity of the people living through it all. <br /><br />The novel is divided into 40 chapters after a short preface from the author's granddaughter and there are small black and white artful illustrations at each chapter beginning. On the sleeve of the novel is a picture of the author reading this novel to his grandchildren perched on his lap. <br /><br />A Little History of the World reads like a story should be told with the innocence in language of an older time. Though the novel has been revised and translated into English after Gombrich had moved to London right before WWII, it reminds me of books I was read to as a child in structure and style. <br />Because this is an older text and there are things that the author could not possibly have known at the time, it lead to great discussions with my younger audience when reading of the different milestones in the knowledge of things. It was also refreshing to read a take on history that wasn't from the American side of view nor had a large presence in the overall telling. <br />Gombrich used a very fluid way of maneuvering the different times and territories with a large portion on the middle ages and the age of enlightenment. With his Austria - Hungarian heritage, he spent more time on the central focus of Germany (before it was actually Germany) in a geographical sense and the forces surrounding that entire area with all the back and forth of events. <br />This is what I wanted to hear and read about. It's not all about just dates and names and always the same names that pop up. Gombrich touched on the state of the lands and its inhabitants before and after events happened to demonstrate the effects of conquest, peril, defeat or rebuild. Now, by all means, not all of history can be put into a 284-page book, but I tell you what my listeners picked up on really quickly: history repeats itself....over and over. (Some smart kids!)<br /><br />What am I left with at the end of the day after reading this novel?<br /> <br />First, I did some research on the author, and though this is a highly praised book, I am also a bit baffled on how this novel came to be and why. None of this makes it any less or more than it is. With Sir Gombrich's background in the arts and art history, it seems a fair jump to write a book as such and I feel enlightened about reading it. <br /><br />My take away would be that I am humbled by the author's voice in humanity and by the end of the novel, after revisions and add ons at his older age, I appreciate his point of view over the atrocities humans have committed and created and there's a bit of personal wisdom shared that moved me along the way of reading, especially a the end. <br /><br />Is this an in-depth history? No. <br />Is it enough of an overview? I would say no. <br />Can it be used as an intro to history? Yes, it could, but the audience should be upper elementary or older in my opinion. <br /><br />It's a great and gentle introduction from a different perspective not easily found otherwise. Perfect as additional reading or read aloud. <br /><br />Enjoy :) <br /><br />More of my reviews here:<br /><a href="https://scarlettreadzandrunz.com/" rel="nofollow noopener">Through Novel Time &amp; Distance</a><br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="9421950187992961426" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        rated it

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2440852396" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18088536483466246743">Well worth reading, and, in many ways, plenty entertaining and informative, but ... to my mind ... more interesting to read as a period piece than as a historical work (for a number of reasons).<br/><br/>So many things to treasure: the story of how the book came to be written is fascinating (and kudos to Gombrich for undertaking and completing the work which proved the springboard to a rich and successful career); the voice-tone-and-audience combination is intriguing - this isn't your normal "children's b</span>
  <span id="freeText18088536483466246743" style="display:none">Well worth reading, and, in many ways, plenty entertaining and informative, but ... to my mind ... more interesting to read as a period piece than as a historical work (for a number of reasons).<br /><br />So many things to treasure: the story of how the book came to be written is fascinating (and kudos to Gombrich for undertaking and completing the work which proved the springboard to a rich and successful career); the voice-tone-and-audience combination is intriguing - this isn't your normal "children's book," but it is thought-provoking to think about such a broad sweep of history through the lens of <i>telling the world's story</i> to a young person - more than anything, the book takes a very different approach to the many history classes and texts that I watched my offspring navigate; and ... well ... the scope - just <i>think about it</i> - telling the world's history (efficiently, effectively, in under 300 pages) without resorting to lists or dry factual recitations - and being able to do so with a heavy emphasis on context and cross-referencing prior content; and all of it packaged in bite-sized chapters (or perfect for one-before-bedtime consumption) and presented in digestible, comfortable prose.... All very impressive ...<br /><br />But ... but ... with ambition comes the risk of being constrained by reality .... And so, ... how else to say it ... maybe (just maybe) the history of the world can't (or, I dunno, <i>shouldn't</i>) be told in under 300 pages? And maybe (just maybe) the book more accurately <i> should</i> have been called <i>A Little <b>Euro-centric</b> History of the World</i>.... Yes, yes, Gombrich devotes a handful of chapters to China and, from time to time, mentions Japan and India in passing. But Africa gets short shrift and South America ... <i>South America!</i> (think Brazil, Argentina, Chile, Peru, Columbia .... and Uruguay and Venezuela and Ecuador and...) is largely (if not entirely) absent. The Panama Canal, Australia, Canada ... nah ... these don't seem like small omissions to me ... OK, OK, I know you can't do everything, but .... but .... but maybe that didn't matter to a European reader 80 years ago, and maybe it doesn't matter to European readers today ... but for <i>this</i> American reader who travels (and works) globally, it undercuts the title and, to some extent, dilutes the achievement.<br /><br />The closing chapter, written 5o (fifty?) years after the book's original publication, adds great value for modern readers. Indeed, part of me thinks it might have been more effective as a forward or updated preface, but it is what it is. I understand that the modern text we read today is "revised," but it's unclear where or how, except for a few notable exceptions, including one (very significant and well-placed) parenthetical. But, as helpful and appropriate the revisions and updates may have been, they only make the omissions (noted above) all the more glaring (to my eyes).<br /><br />Sadly, it's hard to read this book - or for that matter, history, generally - without becoming increasingly sour and skeptical about organized religion. (I'm not talking about faith or belief, I'm merely highlighting the frequency with which, across the span of history, human life is taken in a higher being's name.) In this context, Gombrich did a more than adequate job of exposing the hypocrisy of hate and greed and empire that, alas, so commonly defines human history. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="18088536483466246743" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_568951974" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/568951974" />
    <a title="Kathleen" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/13954899-kathleen"><img alt="Kathleen" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1365166675p2/13954899.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/568951974?book_show_action=true">Mar 23, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kathleen" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kathleen" href="/user/show/13954899-kathleen">Kathleen</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/13954899-kathleen?shelf=audio">audio</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/13954899-kathleen?shelf=ya">ya</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/13954899-kathleen?shelf=history-quasi">history-quasi</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer568951974" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3756463203252392361">I first read the book and then — purely because I liked it — listened to the Blackstone Audio app, narrated capably by Ralph Cosham. I like his British accent. He is easy on the ears. Could listen to him for hours. Do, in fact.<br/><br/> Need a gift for an adolescent or young adult, or just want to learn a little more about world history yourself? I suggest this book, if you don't mind a grandfatherly tone addressed to adolescents. Some say the conversational voice is condescending, but I took no insult a</span>
  <span id="freeText3756463203252392361" style="display:none">I first read the book and then — purely because I liked it — listened to the Blackstone Audio app, narrated capably by Ralph Cosham. I like his British accent. He is easy on the ears. Could listen to him for hours. Do, in fact.<br /><br /> Need a gift for an adolescent or young adult, or just want to learn a little more about world history yourself? I suggest this book, if you don't mind a grandfatherly tone addressed to adolescents. Some say the conversational voice is condescending, but I took no insult and enjoyed it. As for the YA tag, I learned a lot, even as an adult.<br /><br />And for those of us who read historicals set in the Napoleonic Wars, that chapter offers a reality check. Those were grim years, with starvation, death, and desperation rampant, for all the derring-do of the Duke of Wellington, Admiral Lord Nelson, and their merry bands of dashing spies, sailors, and soldiers. <br /><br />First, the bad news: The book begins with cave man (which is good, but sadly, it's gender skewed towards "man" throughout). Furthermore, it focuses almost entirely on the Western Civilizations, including England, Egypt, France, Germany, etc., and of course the Romans and Greeks, with limited excursions into China, India, etc. Minimal mention of Manifest Destiny and the devastation of the natives in the New World. Also, it's a survey, an overview text, so it's broad, not deep. <br /><br />But for all that, I actually enjoyed this small "History for Dummies" and gained a fair grounding in the basic historical timeline from it. The focus is more on overarching historical trends, less on names and dates. It was written by an Austrian, a Jewish art historian who overlaps history with art and culture (I liked that). The author lightly peppers the book with anecdotes and offers some unique perspectives.<br /><br /> Published originally in 1935, with a revised edition published by Yale University Press in 2005, the biggest offense might be Gombrich's (at the time, ~1935) ignorance of the coming offenses of Hitler's Nazi atrocities. However, he does include it in his more recently added / updated final chapter (see comment for more on this). Gombrich made other mistakes (particularly when discussing President Wilson's 1918 proffered peace treaty and Germany's response to it), but in his 2005 revised final chapter he admits his error and clarifies the confusion. Fair enough, and decent role modeling. <br /><br />It's a best-seller, translated in many languages. I treasure it. I have the hardback and also the recording by Blackstone Audio, narrated by Ralph Cosham. His narration is wonderful. </span>
  <a data-text-id="3756463203252392361" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

Ernst Gombrich is a pleasure to read because he's not trying to sound academic. <div id="review_1031435549" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1031435549" />
    <a title="Aaron Wolfson" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4463803-aaron-wolfson"><img alt="Aaron Wolfson" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1288072528p2/4463803.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1031435549?book_show_action=true">Aug 19, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Aaron Wolfson" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Aaron Wolfson" href="/user/show/4463803-aaron-wolfson">Aaron Wolfson</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4463803-aaron-wolfson?shelf=non-fiction">non-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4463803-aaron-wolfson?shelf=own">own</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4463803-aaron-wolfson?shelf=history">history</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4463803-aaron-wolfson?shelf=reference">reference</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4463803-aaron-wolfson?shelf=kids">kids</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1031435549" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15217167318896948577">This is a book that attempts to explain world history in its entirety. It is targeted for kids, it was written in six weeks in the 1930s, and it was only translated into English 70 years later (the author insisted on doing it himself).<br/><br/>It's inevitable that a 280-page history of the world will have issues. And in fact it has several inherent limitations and flaws. Here are the biggest ones:<br/><br/> 1. The author is an Austrian expatriate living in England. Therefore, the history is wildly biased toward We</span>
  <span id="freeText15217167318896948577" style="display:none">This is a book that attempts to explain world history in its entirety. It is targeted for kids, it was written in six weeks in the 1930s, and it was only translated into English 70 years later (the author insisted on doing it himself).<br /><br />It's inevitable that a 280-page history of the world will have issues. And in fact it has several inherent limitations and flaws. Here are the biggest ones:<br /><br /> 1. The author is an Austrian expatriate living in England. Therefore, the history is wildly biased toward Western Europe. While India, China, the Middle East, and the Americas get some time, you will only get the highest-level overviews for them. There's next to nothing about Africa.<br /> <br /> 2. Since it's aimed at kids, some negative things are glossed over. At least they're mentioned, so you get a sense of how much of history has been a tale of the powerful running rampant over the powerless. And the final chapter, written recently, attempts to expiate this.<br /><br /> 3. The tone and diction get repetitive after a while. You will never want to use the word "dreadful" again.<br /><br /> 4. You'll probably be annoyed by the curt summaries of events that you know are much more complex. For instance, the buildup to Caesar's Civil War is summed up as, "[after conquering Gaul] Caesar turned his army toward Rome." Uh, yeah, but...<br /><br /> 5. It seems to be written from a Christian bias, and it has an interesting theory on how Christianity got humanity through the Dark ages.<br /><br />But I still say the book is worth reading. It's fast and easy, and you'll probably learn something you didn't know, and find several jumping-off points for further study. I did, anyway. The phrase "sweep of history" is totally overused, but I'm not sure what other phrase to use for something like this: the point is, from reading this book you really do feel that -- whatever you want to call it.<br /><br />What I really want now is a critical companion to this book that goes through and fact-checks and supports each chapter. Maybe it exists already!</span>
  <a data-text-id="15217167318896948577" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_953850461" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/953850461" />
    <a title="Morgane" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7308397-morgane"><img alt="Morgane" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1510098605p2/7308397.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/953850461?book_show_action=true">May 31, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Morgane" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Morgane" href="/user/show/7308397-morgane">Morgane</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer953850461" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12632070106517985977">It's important to keep in mind that his target audience is children/young adults. He writes in a simple style, often engaging the reader directly ("Why do you think this happened?"), and painting a very general picture of world (read: European) history. While this probably won't shake the core of your beliefs about history, Gombrich does do a good job of humanizing all cultures and showing how even events in ancient history are still relevant. <br/><br/>As for it being Eurocentric, well, that's not surpri</span>
  <span id="freeText12632070106517985977" style="display:none">It's important to keep in mind that his target audience is children/young adults. He writes in a simple style, often engaging the reader directly ("Why do you think this happened?"), and painting a very general picture of world (read: European) history. While this probably won't shake the core of your beliefs about history, Gombrich does do a good job of humanizing all cultures and showing how even events in ancient history are still relevant. <br /><br />As for it being Eurocentric, well, that's not surprising. He's European after all. I did appreciate that he fully acknowledged the great achievements of non-European cultures, and that he straight up said that what Europeans did to Native Americans was unspeakably horrible, and one of the worst chapters of our own history. Most historians like to gloss over that.<br /><br />The final chapter, written in a later edition, was particularly interesting. At the time when he was writing the first edition, Hitler was rising to power, but no one yet anticipated how cruel he would be. Gombrich had even made claims that surely we were now living in an age where religious persecution was a thing of the past. This last chapter reflects on that previous statement, and he comments on how circular history can be, if we don't learn from it. I think this is the most important reason to study history: how can you know where you're going if you don't know where you've been?</span>
  <a data-text-id="12632070106517985977" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer12167929446550108205">Gombrich is an amazing storyteller. This is a great introduction to the history of the (European and Mediterranean) world. The author doesn't give many historical dates, and that was a wise choice because the story flows like an epic poem (yes, at times the style is very poetic):<br/><br/><i>"The dark clouds on the horizon are the storm clouds of the Migrations, and it was in those forests, beside the rivers, that the first monks converted and educated the Germanic tribes."</i><br/> <br/>After reading this book the strong</span>
  <span id="freeText12167929446550108205" style="display:none">Gombrich is an amazing storyteller. This is a great introduction to the history of the (European and Mediterranean) world. The author doesn't give many historical dates, and that was a wise choice because the story flows like an epic poem (yes, at times the style is very poetic):<br /><br /><i>"The dark clouds on the horizon are the storm clouds of the Migrations, and it was in those forests, beside the rivers, that the first monks converted and educated the Germanic tribes."</i><br /> <br />After reading this book the strongest feeling I'm left with is disappointment. It seems like we, human beings, never learn. We make the same mistakes again and again, we just think we can get away with it "this time". What's sadly recurrent is always thinking we are better than others, thinking we know better than others, thinking we deserve more than others, and (sometimes) wanting to give others what we see as the best in our narrow-minded worldview, and give it by force if necessary. <br /><br />Gombrich said he knew a wise old Buddhist who <i>"once said he'd love to know why someone who boasts that he's the cleverest, the strongest, the bravest and the most gifted man on earth is thought ridiculous and embarrassing, whereas if, instead of "I", he says, "we are the most intelligent, the strongest, the bravest and the most gifted people on earth", his fellow countrymen applaud enthusiastically and call him a patriot. For there is nothing patriotic about it."</i></span>

  <span id="freeText17972570191212882160" style="display:none">It certainly IS a world history - well mainly European history - for young readers, meaning it's an easy read and all facts are very well and basically explained. Actually it rather feels like someone tells you a story rather than yet another boring history lesson.<br /><br />What I really loved about this way to access our history was how easily he connected the historical events with one another and here lies one of the strengths of this book. It is a story of developping mankind. You can experience how people got aware of themselves, how giant leaders rose and failed and yet how it is all the benefits and failures of humanity who shaped our history. Especially the last chapter about populismn is really worth reading these days, even though of course it reflected on the 1930s rather than on our days. After all we are only humans - but with books like this we hopefully take the chance to learn from our mistakes!<br />Bravo!<br /><br />So why not 5 stars then? The missing star is because of the somewhat old fashioned language. It is my grandfather telling me things and it thus feels a bit moldy. With an up to date voice this would easily be a five star book to me!</span>

<div id="review_931778558" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/931778558" />

      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer931778558" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15973351608600211339">Great book. This is how history should be told. The history of the world, from Creation to World War II, is told a series of stories. The author doesn't shy away from telling history as he understands it. This is especially true when he gets to the history he lived through. Yet, in the final chapter of the book, he goes back and corrects things that he later felt like he misunderstood at the time of living through it. <br/><br/>The book is written for younger children, maybe 9-11, but I read it aloud to m</span>
  <span id="freeText15973351608600211339" style="display:none">Great book. This is how history should be told. The history of the world, from Creation to World War II, is told a series of stories. The author doesn't shy away from telling history as he understands it. This is especially true when he gets to the history he lived through. Yet, in the final chapter of the book, he goes back and corrects things that he later felt like he misunderstood at the time of living through it. <br /><br />The book is written for younger children, maybe 9-11, but I read it aloud to my older children (13 and 16) and we all thoroughly enjoyed it.<br /><br />If you have younger children, this is a great book to read to them each morning as part of your homeschool. The chapters are brief, and there are only forty of them, so you can get through the whole thing in about two months' time. It is actually a part of a larger series of books, the "A Little History..." series, so you can move on from this one to the one on science or art. That is what I am planning to do with my children.<br /><br />Although...honestly...my kids are just an excuse for me to read these books.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15973351608600211339" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <a title="Suad Shamma" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/11245979-suad-shamma"><img alt="Suad Shamma" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1507364224p2/11245979.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1276413553?book_show_action=true">May 10, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Suad Shamma" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Suad Shamma" href="/user/show/11245979-suad-shamma">Suad Shamma</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/11245979-suad-shamma?shelf=2015">2015</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1276413553" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9348171701294319499">I did not enjoy this book at all. Going into it I had expected to thoroughly enjoy expanding my knowledge on the history of the world in brief little chapters. However, I quickly realized, as soon as I started, that this book just wasn't for me. <br/><br/>It is too simplistic - in a childish manner - and obviously written for a much younger audience. I found that to be a shame, because although you mean this to be a book aimed at children, you had this great opportunity to write a book that could somehow,</span>
  <span id="freeText9348171701294319499" style="display:none">I did not enjoy this book at all. Going into it I had expected to thoroughly enjoy expanding my knowledge on the history of the world in brief little chapters. However, I quickly realized, as soon as I started, that this book just wasn't for me. <br /><br />It is too simplistic - in a childish manner - and obviously written for a much younger audience. I found that to be a shame, because although you mean this to be a book aimed at children, you had this great opportunity to write a book that could somehow, someway, cater to all age groups, and instead you wrote it in a way that comes off as condescending and patronizing. <br /><br />The way he keeps asking questions right in the middle of a historical account, jars you and takes you out of it. <br /><br />I think Gombrich could've done a lot more with the material he had, and that the book could've been more than it turned out to be. Disappointing. Definitely don't recommend reading it unless you're below 10 years of age. <br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="9348171701294319499" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_63586630" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/63586630" />
    <a title="karen" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/37212-karen"><img alt="karen" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1179860464p2/37212.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/63586630?book_show_action=true">Jul 15, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="karen" class="user" itemprop="url" name="karen" href="/user/show/37212-karen">karen</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer63586630" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8765542430987171009">I'm a little biased since I work for the publisher, but it still is a remarkable book. Gombrich was hired by a friend/publisher to translate a short children's history of the world into German and said he could write something better; it became this book. He set about writing a chapter a week by researching, writing, then reading the chapter to his future wife while on walks. It originally was published before WWII; the Nazis banned it. After the war Gombrich added some chapters. Gombrich later </span>
  <span id="freeText8765542430987171009" style="display:none">I'm a little biased since I work for the publisher, but it still is a remarkable book. Gombrich was hired by a friend/publisher to translate a short children's history of the world into German and said he could write something better; it became this book. He set about writing a chapter a week by researching, writing, then reading the chapter to his future wife while on walks. It originally was published before WWII; the Nazis banned it. After the war Gombrich added some chapters. Gombrich later wrote the very successful art history text, The Story of Art (1950)(<a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/The_Story_of_Art">http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/The_Stor...</a>). You can hear his granddaughter speaking about the book in this NPR interview <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://www.npr.org/templates/story/story.php?storyId=4980126">http://www.npr.org/templates/story/st...</a>. <br /><br />I think the writing is beautiful. It's not comprehensive, but does give a sense of the world to young and old.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8765542430987171009" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText10976829251787340630" style="display:none">There is no book ever in the world that could cover the entire history of the world and still be able to please everyone. So I imagine Gombrich’s attempt is no different. It is an useful primer to introduce children to salient events of history. It must be remembered it is quite eurocentric.
