    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_2386728269" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2386728269" />
    <a title="Paula Sarah" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/38574202-paula-sarah"><img alt="Paula Sarah" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1569953499p2/38574202.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2386728269?book_show_action=true">Aug 17, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Paula Sarah" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Paula Sarah" href="/user/show/38574202-paula-sarah">Paula Sarah</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780300143324" href="/book/show/3711671-a-little-history-of-the-world">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/38574202-paula-sarah?shelf=history">history</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/38574202-paula-sarah?shelf=non-fiction">non-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2386728269" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3355876856193996448">I’m gonna be frank: I hated this. Absolutely hated it. This is not a good book, and it saddens me to see so many people completely over the moon with it. Please stay and listen to me explain to you why. I’m gonna try to be less condescending than E. H. Gombrich.<br/><br/>Disclaimer, as it will become relevant shortly: I am German. The edition I am reading is English. Okay. Let’s move on.<br/><br/>This book has a serious branding problem, apart from just being bad and dangerous.<br/>It’s clearly written for children — an</span>
  <span id="freeText3355876856193996448" style="display:none">I’m gonna be frank: I hated this. Absolutely hated it. This is not a good book, and it saddens me to see so many people completely over the moon with it. Please stay and listen to me explain to you why. I’m gonna try to be less condescending than E. H. Gombrich.<br /><br />Disclaimer, as it will become relevant shortly: I am German. The edition I am reading is English. Okay. Let’s move on.<br /><br />This book has a serious branding problem, apart from just being bad and dangerous.<br />It’s clearly written for children — and young ones at that — but looks like a book grown-ass people, who take themselves seriously and respect themselves, would want to buy. This, however, is partly (!) due to the English translation. It was originally published in German as “Eine kurze Weltgeschichte für junge Leser” - A Short History of the World for Young Readers. Why the decision was made to change the title in this translation? No clue. Mistake, if you ask me.<br />However, even with this in mind, I still disagree with the need for condescension the author apparently saw. I believe that you can talk to children like you don’t think they’re stupid and they will usually understand you pretty well.<br /><br />Okay so, this book is supposed to be for children, and as an adult, you will feel like you’re being talked down to. However, I don’t think you should read it to your children either. Here’s why: <br /><br />The view of “world” history presented in this book is unfortunately — though sadly not surprisingly — euro-centric and, frankly, ignorant. Calling it “World History” is, in my opinion, not super accurate. The book perpetuates the idea held in the “West” that only we made history, and that all the great and important feats of history were ours, and then China also did two things. African history ends after the part about Egypt (with maybe a mention in the part about how great the Romans were). Half a page on scientific discoveries in the “Arab” world in the obligatory Islam chapter (that’s what it felt like) but a whole chapter on how some monks built a few monasteries and converted the Germanic tribes, and then a dozen more chapters about Europe, until Columbus “discovered” America and the “Indians”, of course. Yeah, not kidding.  Sadly, “This chapter in the history of mankind is so appalling and shameful to us Europeans that I would rather not say anything more about it.” While the author mentions how Cortez slaughtered Mexicans, who had taken him in, and thereby “demonstrated his true courage”(p.179), talking more about the atrocities committed by Europeans in the name of Colonialism and their countries is apparently way too painful for him. Not a word about the Native American genocide, just “British and Spanish settlers had meanwhile pressed on towards the west, fighting Indian tribes as they went. You must have read books about cowboys and Indians, so you’ll know what it was like” (p.253). The way the subject of slavery was dealt with is outrageous — the “negro slaves […] were very badly treated” (p.253). Yeah. Bit of a damn understatement, don’t you think? Come to think of it, Gombrich also uses “very badly treated” (p.265) when talking about Colonialism. Maybe that’s just his preferred euphemism for murder motivated by racism. But by all means, protect the children from ever finding out Europeans ever did anything wrong. Also, mentioning twice that the Chinese invented something before the Europeans did, and then continuing to only talk about how the Europeans used it, and in general only talking about things that happened outside of Europe if you really couldn’t not mention it does not make your book World History.  If I wrote down all the ignorant, and sometimes racist things said in this book, my review would be even longer, so I will refrain from doing that.<br /><br />(2) I also take issue with the outdated theist remarks in the book. The author pretty much assumes that only small, perfect Christian children will read the book, and tells them they shouldn’t worry, if they haven’t already, he’s sure they will read the Bible soon enough. What do we say to that? “Disgusting.”<br />For reference: Especially in the chapter about Christianity (“The Good News”), Gombrich writes “You probably know the essentials of what [Jesus] taught” — which prompted me to ask myself whether small Muslim, Jewish, Buddhist or Sikh […] children who had not been taught anything about Christianity were not allowed to read this book. It’s not that he doesn’t explain what Jesus was all about, it’s the smug assumption that anyone who would read this book would already be well-versed in it. I didn’t grow up religious, and at, say, the age of 10, I wouldn’t have known exactly what Jesus taught even though I wasn’t even part of any other religious group.<br /><br />(3) Women don’t get to make history. The first woman (I believe) mentioned by name is Cleopatra — the next one is Joan of Arc, almost a hundred pages later. (I think a total of three women are mentioned by name…)<br />Anyway, Cleopatra is “seduced” by Caesar and then that’s that. Caesar has Egypt now. <br />It’s obvious that women, historically haven’t been allowed to make history. I’m not trying to criticise the author for centuries of oppression; it’s not his fault. All I ask is that he doesn’t present women as passive pawns in a man’s history of the world. Give me like two sentences about how smart Cleopatra was, her achievements, and don’t make the only mention of her how “beautiful” she was, how Caesar had sex with her and then suddenly Egypt was his. Don’t let your children read this sexist crap.<br /><br />(4) Factual inaccuracies. I was a History major in High School (you can do that in Germany, it’s basically an advanced course and you get to pick two of them), and am therefore annoyingly well-versed in German history. In Chapter 35, Gombrich states that on the third day of the Völkerschlacht bei Leipzig, the Bavarians abandoned Napoleon’s forces and changed sides to fight with the allied armies from the different German kingdoms (cf. p. 237). It wasn’t the Bavarians, though. It was the Saxons (3.000 to 4.000 of them), and about 500 soldiers from Wuerttemberg. <br />I don’t know, there might be more. I just happened to notice this one because I am a nerd about this crap.<br /><br />While I have said very mean (but true) things about this book so far, I have to mention that in the last chapter, “Looking Back”, added in the early 2000s, I believe, the author addresses the Holocaust and the Nazis and calls on the children reading the book to be tolerant and not to discriminate against people who might be “different in some way” (p.276). Good on you.<br /><br />Okay, now watch me get really nitpicky. There are a few issues related to “Germany”, “Germans”, “Germanic tribes”, Germany and Germans. Some of them might be due to translation. Let me explain:<br />In the earlier parts of history, especially Roman occupation of Europe (or “Germania”, the Roman name for the part of Europe inhabited by the Germanic tribes), the author uses the terms “Germanic tribes” and “Germans” interchangeably. This is very misleading, and modern historians discourage using “Germans” as a noun for “Germanic tribes” because there simply is no noun for the “Germanen” in the English language and actual Germans aren’t even the majority of descendants of the Germanic tribes. It is also discouraged, I believe, because during the rise of Nazism, the Germanic tribes were used to incite racism and ideas of their own supremacy in the German population, as Gombrich states himself on page 277.<br />The term “Germany” is used in the context of the Middle Ages without a mention of the fact that Germany wasn’t a country back then. Yes, there was a King of the Germans (don’t ask me why; it’s only called that in English) but the fact is that the Holy Roman Empire of the German Nation wasn’t just what we now call Germany but also parts of Italy, for example. Fact is, there was no country called “Germany” back then, and while “deutsche Lande” — “German countries / German counties…” were found mentioned as far back as the 14th century, the word “Deutschland” — “Germany” was only mentioned in the 15th century, much later than Gombrich uses it in his book (“Cities and Citizens”).<br />He also states that the Holy Roman Empire of the German Nation ends “before the [9th] century was out” (p.129) after Charlemagne’s death even though Otto I. was crowned Emperor in 962 A.D. and the Empire is sometimes debated to have only started with his coronation. It went on for centuries and ended only in 1806 during the Napoleonic Wars. Annoyingly, Gombrich then writes exactly that in Chapter 35 (cf. p. 231), contradicting himself. <br />It should also be noted that Gombrich carries on calling people the “German emperor” even though the empire supposedly ended in 800-something A.D. and “[n]ot even the name remained”. (p.129). Confusing? Yes.<br />Then, in Chapter 38, he say that “he’s known people who were alive before […] Germany existed”, despite having used the term “Germany” to describe (presumably!) the Holy Roman Empire of the German Nation for the last hundred pages. The thing is, we’ll never know if he was referring to that Empire or the geographical place of the country we know as Germany today (or maybe even the place inside the borders of the German Empire founded in 1871) because he just won’t tell us. <br />Imagine how confusing all of this would be to someone who hasn’t spent the better part of three years studying this.<br />I’m especially confused as to why this was so hard to understand for Gombrich because he grew up in Austria and Austrian history of that time is in large part intertwined with German history (I’m referring especially to the German revolution of 1848 and the German Question: When the revolutionaries tried to establish a German state, they debated whether Austria-Hungary should be part of it, as Austrians were seen as German. You see, they used the German language to establish who counted as German. This is exactly the subject Gombrich writes so misleadingly about and, I’m hoping, at least, he would have dealt with it in school as it concerned Austria as well as Germany.)<br /><br />(I’ve spent the better part of an hour googling German etymology in German and English. I wouldn’t be this nitpicky usually but I’ve been socially distancing for six months, I have more time than I could possibly need, and most importantly, I am annoyed.)<br /><br />I started out this book, and this review annoyed but the further I got, the more outraged I became. This book is outdated and whitewashes history. It is outrageous to me that I could buy this book in the bookstore of the Metropolitan Museum of Art in the year 2018, and that there are presumably people out there who read this to their children.<br /><br />This review is now almost as long as my History Abitur.<br /><br />In summary: It bad.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3355876856193996448" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

//<![CDATA[
      var newTip = new Tip($('bookCover_6161407'), "\n\n  <h2><a class=\"readable bookTitle\" href=\"https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6161407-art-work?from_choice=false&amp;from_home_module=false\">ART/WORK: Everything You Need to Know (and Do) As You Pursue Your Art Career<\/a><\/h2>\n\n      <div>\n        by <a class=\"authorName\" href=\"/author/show/2808210.Heather_Darcy_Bhandari?a=9&amp;origin=related_works\">Heather Darcy Bhandari<\/a>\n      <\/div>\n\n          <div class=\"smallText uitext darkGreyText\">\n            <span class=\"minirating\"><span class=\"stars staticStars notranslate\"><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p3\"><\/span><\/span> 4.21 avg rating &mdash; 1,062 ratings<\/span>            &mdash; published 2009\n          <\/div>\n\n    <div class=\"addBookTipDescription\">\n      \n<span id=\"freeTextContainer4486271169458349065\"> Find Out What They Didn\'t Teach You in Art School   The most comprehensive guide of its kind, Art/Work gives artists of every level the tools they need to make it in an art world so competitive one dealer likens it to \"The Sopranos, except nobody ge<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText4486271169458349065\" style=\"display:none\"> Find Out What They Didn\'t Teach You in Art School   The most comprehensive guide of its kind, Art/Work gives artists of every level the tools they need to make it in an art world so competitive one dealer likens it to &quot;The Sopranos, except nobody gets killed.&quot; Whether you\'re an art school grad looking for a gallery, a mid-career artist managing a busy studio, or someone just thinking about becoming a professional artist, this indispensable resource will help you build your career and protect yourself along the way.  Unlike other creative professionals, visual artists don\'t have agents or managers. You have to do it all yourself, at least until you find gallery representation -- and even then, there are important business and legal issues you need to understand to stay in control of your career and ensure you\'re being treated fairly. Heather Darcy Bhandari, a gallery director, and Jonathan Melber, an arts lawyer, walk you through these issues so that you can essentially act as your own manager and agent. They show you, for example, how to tackle business basics such as tracking inventory and preparing invoices; how to take legal precautions like registering a copyright and drafting consignment forms; how to use promotional tools like websites and business cards; and how to approach career decisions such as choosing the right venue to show your work.  In addition to drawing on their own experiences, Bhandari and Melber interviewed nearly one hundred curators, dealers, and other arts professionals, in cities across the country, about what they expect from and look for in artists. The authors also talked to a host of artists about their careers and the lessons they\'ve learned navigating the art world. The book is full of their entertaining anecdotes and candid advice.  No matter what kind of artist you are -- or want to be -- this book will help you. Encompassing a grand sweep of human history, A History of the World in 100 Objects begins with one of the earliest surviving objects made by human hands, a chopping tool from the Olduvai gorge in Africa, and ends with objects which characterise the world we live in today. Seen through MacGregor\'s eyes, history is a kaleidoscope - shifting, interconnected, constantly surprising, and shaping our world today in ways that most of us have never imagined. A stone pillar tells us about a great Indian emperor preaching tolerance to his people; Spanish pieces of eight tell us about the beginning of a global currency; and an early Victorian tea-set speaks to us about the impact of empire. He also addresses the phenomenon of art critiques as a microcosm for teaching art as a whole and dissects real-life critiques, highlighting presuppositions and dynamics that make them confusing and suggesting ways to make them more helpful.\n\nElkins\'s no-nonsense approach clears away the assumptions about art instruction that are not borne out by classroom practice. For example, he notes that despite much talk about instilling visual acuity and teaching technique, in practice neither teachers nor students behave as if those were their principal goals. He addresses the absurdity of pretending that sexual issues are absent from life-drawing classes and questions the practice of holding up great masters and masterpieces as models for students capable of producing only mediocre art. He also discusses types of art -- including art that takes time to complete and art that isn\'t serious -- that cannot be learned in studio art classes.\n\nElkins\'s incisive commentary illuminates the experience of learning art for those involved in it, while opening an intriguing window for those outside the discipline.\n\n<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"5178408221240590026\" href=\"#\" <span id="freeTextContainer4184334059894885974"><i>Sir Ernst Hans Josef Gombrich</i>, OM, CBE (30 March 1909 – 3 November 2001) was an Austrian-born art historian, who spent most of his working life in the United Kingdom.<br/><br/></span>

These were the concerns of Socrates, who spent his days in the ancient Athenian marketplace asking awkward questions, disconcerting the people he met by showing them how little they genuinely understood. This engaging book introduces the great thinkers in Western philosophy and explores their most compelling ideas about the world and how best to live in it.\n\nIn forty brief chapters, Nigel Warburton guides us on a chronological tour of the major ideas in the history of philosophy. He provides interesting and often quirky stories of the lives and deaths of thought-provoking philosophers from Socrates, who chose to die by hemlock poisoning rather than live on without the freedom to think for himself, to Peter Singer, who asks the disquieting philosophical and ethical questions that haunt our own times.\n\nWarburton not only makes philosophy accessible, he offers inspiration to think, argue, reason, and ask in the tradition of Socrates. A Little History of Philosophy presents the grand sweep of humanity\'s search for philosophical understanding and invites all to join in the discussion.<\/span>\n  <a He has researched, taught, and written <\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText6882098559692274396\" style=\"display:none\">This \'little history\' takes on a very big subject: the glorious span of literature from Greek myth to graphic novels, from The Epic of Gilgamesh to Harry Potter. John Sutherland is perfectly suited to the task. He has researched, taught, and written on virtually every area of literature, and his infectious passion for books and reading has defined his own life. Now he guides young readers and the grown-ups in their lives on an entertaining journey \'through the wardrobe\' to a greater awareness of how literature from across the world can transport us and help us to make sense of what it means to be human.\n \nSutherland introduces great classics in his own irresistible way, enlivening his offerings with humor as well as learning: Beowulf, Shakespeare, Don Quixote, the Romantics, Dickens, Moby Dick, The Waste Land, Woolf, 1984, and dozens of others. He adds to these a less-expected, personal selection of authors and works, including literature usually considered well below \'serious attention\' - from the rude jests of Anglo-Saxon runes to The Da Vinci Code. With masterful digressions into various themes - censorship, narrative tricks, self-publishing, taste, creativity, and madness - Sutherland demonstrates the full depth and intrigue of reading. For younger readers, he offers a proper introduction to literature, promising to interest as much as instruct. For more experienced readers, he promises just the same.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"6882098559692274396\" href=\"#\" onclick=\"swapContent(\$(this));; return false;\">...more<\/a>\n\n    <\/div>\n\n R<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText13622407934147164779\" style=\"display:none\">For curious readers young and old, a rich and colorful history of religion from humanity’s earliest days to our own contentious times\n\nIn an era of hardening religious attitudes and explosive religious violence, this book offers a welcome antidote. Richard Holloway retells the entire history of religion—from the dawn of religious belief to the twenty-first century—with deepest respect and a keen commitment to accuracy. Writing for those with faith and those without, and especially for young readers, he encourages curiosity and tolerance, accentuates nuance and mystery, and calmly restores a sense of the value of faith.\n \nRanging far beyond the major world religions of Judaism, Islam, Christianity, Buddhism, and Hinduism, Holloway also examines where religious belief comes from, the search for meaning throughout history, today’s fascinations with Scientology and creationism, religiously motivated violence, hostilities between religious people and secularists, and more. It takes readers to the stars through the telescope, as the sun replaces the earth at the center of our universe. It delves beneath the surface of the planet, charts the evolution of chemistry\'s periodic table, introduces the physics that explain electricity, gravity, and the structure of atoms. It recounts the scientific quest that revealed the DNA molecule and opened unimagined new vistas for exploration.\n\nEmphasizing surprising and personal stories of scientists both famous and unsung, A Little History of Science traces the march of science through the centuries. The book opens a window on the exciting and unpredictable nature of scientific activity and describes the uproar that may ensue when scientific findings challenge established ideas. Everyone from young adults to grandparents will be fascinated by the answers uncovered in James West Davidson’s vividly told A Little History of the United States. In 300 fast-moving pages, Davidson guides his readers through 500 years, from the first contact between the two halves of the world to the rise of America as a superpower in an era of atomic perils and diminishing resources.   In short, vivid chapters the book brings to life hundreds of individuals whose stories are part of the larger American story. Pilgrim William Bradford stumbles into an Indian deer trap on his first day in America; Harriet Tubman lets loose a pair of chickens to divert attention from her escaping slaves; the toddler Andrew Carnegie, later an ambitious industrial magnate, gobbles his oatmeal with a spoon in each hand. Such stories are riveting in themselves, but they also spark larger questions to ponder about freedom, equality, and unity in the context of a nation that is, and always has been, remarkably divided Are economic crises inevitable under capitalism? Is government intervention in an economy a helpful approach or a disastrous idea? The word may bring to mind images of golden pharaohs and lost civilizations, or Neanderthal skulls and Ice Age cave art. Archaeology is all of these, but also far more: the only science to encompass the entire span of human history—more than three million years!\n \nThis Little History tells the riveting stories of some of the great archaeologists and their amazing discoveries around the globe: ancient Egyptian tombs, Mayan ruins, the first colonial settlements at Jamestown, mysterious Stonehenge, the incredibly preserved Pompeii, and many, many more. In forty brief, exciting chapters, the book recounts archaeology’s development from its eighteenth-century origins to its twenty-first-century technological advances, including remote sensing capabilities and satellite imagery techniques that have revolutionized the field. Shining light on the most intriguing events in the history of the field, this absolutely up-to-date book illuminates archaeology’s controversies, discoveries, heroes and scoundrels, global sites, and In this charming volume, a narrative history written explicitly for a young audience, expert linguist David Crystal proves why the story of language deserves retelling.\n\nFrom the first words of an infant to the peculiar modern dialect of text messaging, A Little Book of Language ranges widely, revealing language’s myriad intricacies and quirks. In animated fashion, Crystal sheds light on the development of unique linguistic styles, the origins of obscure accents, and the search for the first written word. He discusses the plight of endangered languages, as well as successful cases of linguistic revitalization. Much more than a history, Crystal’s work looks forward to the future of language, exploring the effect of technology on our day-to-day reading, writing, and speech. Through enlightening tables, diagrams, and quizzes, as well as Crystal’s avuncular and entertaining style, A Little Book of Language will reveal the story of language to be a captivating tale for all
